<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Details">Details</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Google_Books&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Details">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Results from Google Books show up in both the universal <a href="/wiki/Google_Search" title="Google Search">Google Search</a> and in the dedicated Google Books search website (<i>books.google.com</i>).
</p><p>In response to search queries, Google Books allows users to view full pages from books in which the search terms appear if the book is out of copyright or if the copyright owner has given permission. If Google believes the book is still under copyright, a user sees "snippets" of text around the queried search terms. All instances of the search terms in the book text appear with a yellow highlight.
</p><p>The four access levels used on Google Books are:<sup id="cite_ref-Google_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Google-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><b>Full view</b>: Books in the <a href="/wiki/Public_domain" title="Public domain">public domain</a> are available for "full view" and can be downloaded for free. In-print books acquired through the Partner Program are also available for full view if the publisher has given permission, although this is rare.</li>
<li><b>Preview</b>: For in-print books where permission has been granted, the number of viewable pages is limited to a "preview" set by a variety of access restrictions and security measures, some based on user-tracking. Usually, the publisher can set the percentage of the book available for preview.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Users are restricted from copying, downloading or printing book previews. A watermark reading "Copyrighted material" appears at the bottom of pages. All books acquired through the Partner Program are available for preview.</li>
<li><b>Snippet view</b>: A 'snippet view' – two to three lines of text surrounding the queried search term – is displayed in cases where Google does not have permission of the copyright owner to display a preview. This could be because Google cannot identify the owner or the owner declined permission. If a search term appears many times in a book, Google displays no more than three snippets, thus preventing the user from viewing too much of the book. Also, Google does not display any snippets for certain reference books, such as dictionaries, where the display of even snippets can harm the market for the work. Google maintains that no permission is required under copyright law to display the snippet view.<sup id="cite_ref-Jonathan_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jonathan-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b>No preview</b>: Google also displays search results for books that have not been digitized. As these books have not been scanned, their text is not searchable and only the <a href="/wiki/Metadata" title="Metadata">metadata</a> such as the title, author, publisher, number of pages, ISBN, subject and copyright information, and in some cases, a table of contents and book summary is available. In effect, this is similar to an online library card catalog.<sup id="cite_ref-book_sources_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-book_sources-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>In response to criticism from groups such as the <a href="/wiki/American_Association_of_Publishers" class="mw-redirect" title="American Association of Publishers">American Association of Publishers</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Authors_Guild" title="Authors Guild">Authors Guild</a>, Google announced an <a href="/wiki/Opt-out" title="Opt-out">opt-out</a> policy in August 2005, through which copyright owners could provide a list of titles that it did not want scanned, and Google would respect the request. Google also stated that it would not scan any in-copyright books between August and 1 November 2005, to provide the owners with the opportunity to decide which books to exclude from the Project. Thus, Google provides a copyright owner with three choices with respect to any work:<sup id="cite_ref-Jonathan_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jonathan-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>It can participate in the Partner Program to make a book available for preview or full view, in which case it would share revenue derived from the display of pages from the work in response to user queries.</li>
<li>It can let Google scan the book under the Library Project and display snippets in response to user queries.</li>
<li>It can opt out of the Library Project, in which case Google will not scan the book. If the book has already been scanned, Google will reset its access level as 'No preview'.</li></ol>
<p>Most scanned works are no longer in print or commercially available.<sup id="cite_ref-pcworldscan_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pcworldscan-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to procuring books from libraries, Google also obtains books from its publisher partners, through the "Partner Program" – designed to help publishers and authors promote their books. Publishers and authors submit either a digital copy of their book in <a href="/wiki/EPUB" title="EPUB">EPUB</a> or <a href="/wiki/Portable_Document_Format" class="mw-redirect" title="Portable Document Format">PDF</a> format, or a print copy to Google, which is made available on Google Books for preview. The publisher can control the percentage of the book available for preview, with the minimum being 20%. They can also choose to make the book fully viewable, and even allow users to download a PDF copy. Books can also be made available for sale on Google Play.<sup id="cite_ref-book_sources_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-book_sources-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Unlike the Library Project, this does not raise any copyright concerns as it is conducted pursuant to an agreement with the publisher. The publisher can choose to withdraw from the agreement at any time.<sup id="cite_ref-Jonathan_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jonathan-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For many books, Google Books displays the original page numbers. However, <a href="/wiki/Tim_Parks" title="Tim Parks">Tim Parks</a>, writing in <i>The New York Review of Books</i> in 2014, noted that Google had stopped providing page numbers for many recent publications (likely the ones acquired through the Partner Program) "presumably in alliance with the publishers, in order to force those of us who need to prepare footnotes to buy paper editions."<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scanning_of_books">Scanning of books</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Google_Books&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Scanning of books">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The project began in 2002 under the codename Project Ocean. Google co-founder <a href="/wiki/Larry_Page" title="Larry Page">Larry Page</a> had always had an interest in digitizing books. When he and <a href="/wiki/Marissa_Mayer" title="Marissa Mayer">Marissa Mayer</a> began experimenting with <a href="/wiki/Book_scanning" title="Book scanning">book scanning</a> in 2002, it took 40 minutes for them to digitize a 300-page book. But soon after the technology had been developed to the extent that scanning operators could scan up to 6000 pages an hour.<sup id="cite_ref-atlantic_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlantic-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Google established designated scanning centers to which books were transported by trucks. The stations could digitize at the rate of 1,000 pages per hour. The books were placed in a custom-built mechanical cradle that adjusted the book spine in place for the scanning. An array of lights and optical instruments were used – including four cameras, two directed at each half of the book, and a range-finding <a href="/wiki/LIDAR" class="mw-redirect" title="LIDAR">LIDAR</a> that overlaid a three-dimensional laser grid on the book's surface to capture the curvature of the paper. A human operator would turn the pages by hand and operate the cameras through a foot pedal. The system was made efficient since there was no need to flatten the book pages or align them perfectly. The crude images were worked upon by de-warping algorithms that used the LIDAR data to process the images. <a href="/wiki/Optical_character_recognition" title="Optical character recognition">Optical character recognition</a> (OCR) software were developed to process the raw images to text. Algorithms were also created to extract page numbers, footnotes, illustrations and diagrams.<sup id="cite_ref-atlantic_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlantic-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many of the books are scanned using a customized <a href="/wiki/Elphel" title="Elphel">Elphel</a> 323 camera<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> at a rate of 1,000 pages per hour.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Patent" title="Patent">patent</a> awarded to Google in 2009 revealed that Google had come up with an innovative system for scanning books that uses two cameras and infrared light to automatically correct for the curvature of pages in a book. By constructing a 3D model of each page and then "de-warping" it, Google is able to present flat-looking pages without having to really make the pages flat, which requires the use of destructive methods such as <a href="/wiki/Book_scanning#Unbinding" title="Book scanning">unbinding</a> or glass plates to individually flatten each page, which is inefficient for large scale scanning.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Website_functionality">Website functionality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Google_Books&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Website functionality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Each book on Google Books has an associated "About this book" page which displays analytical information regarding the book such as a word map of the most used words and phrases, a selection of pages, list of related books, list of scholarly articles and other books that cite the book, and tables of content.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> This information is collated through automated methods, and sometimes data from third-party sources is used.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> This information provides an insight into the book, particularly useful when only a snippet view is available. The list of related books can often contain irrelevant entries.<sup id="cite_ref-Salon_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Salon-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> In some cases, a book summary and information about the author is also displayed. The page also displays bibliographic information, which can be exported as citations in <a href="/wiki/BibTeX" title="BibTeX">BibTeX</a>, <a href="/wiki/EndNote" title="EndNote">EndNote</a> and <a href="/wiki/RefMan" class="mw-redirect" title="RefMan">RefMan</a> formats. Registered users logged in with their <a href="/wiki/Google_account" class="mw-redirect" title="Google account">Google accounts</a> can post reviews for books on this page. Google Books also displays reviews from <a href="/wiki/Goodreads" title="Goodreads">Goodreads</a> alongside these reviews.<sup id="cite_ref-Salon_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Salon-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> For books still in print, the site provides links to the website of the publisher and booksellers.
</p><p>Google Books can retrieve scanned books from <a href="/wiki/Uniform_resource_locator" class="mw-redirect" title="Uniform resource locator">URLs</a> based on the <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>, <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress_Control_Number" title="Library of Congress Control Number">LCCN</a> and <a href="/wiki/Online_Computer_Library_Center" class="mw-redirect" title="Online Computer Library Center">OCLC</a> record numbers. The 'About this book' page of a book with the ISBN123456789 can be linked as <i>books.google.com/books?vid=ISBN123456789</i>. For some books, Google also provides the ability to link directly to the front cover, title page, copyright page, table of contents, index, and back cover of a book, by using an appropriate parameter. For example, the front cover of a book with the OCLC number 17546826 can be linked as <i>books.google.com/books?vid=OCLC17546826&amp;printsec=frontcover</i><sup id="cite_ref-books-help_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books-help-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Signed-in users can create a personalized collection or a "library" of books, using the "My Library" feature. Organized through "bookshelves", books can be added to the library using a button that appears along with search results or from the "Overview" page of books. The library can be shared with friends by making bookshelves publicly visible and sharing the private library URL. Users can also import a list of books to the library using their <a href="/wiki/ISBN" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN">ISBN</a> or <a href="/wiki/ISSN" class="mw-redirect" title="ISSN">ISSN</a> numbers. There are four default bookshelves which cannot be renamed: "Favorites", "Reading now", "To read" and "Have read".<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The library also has default bookshelves ("Purchased", "Reviewed", "My Books on Google Play", "Recently viewed", "Browsing history", and "Books for you")  to which books get added automatically. Users cannot add or remove books from these bookshelves.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ngram_Viewer">Ngram Viewer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Google_Books&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Ngram Viewer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Google_Ngram_Viewer" title="Google Ngram Viewer">Google Ngram Viewer</a></div>
<p>The Ngram Viewer is a service connected to Google Books that graphs the frequency of word usage across their book collection. The service is important for historians and linguists as it can provide an inside look into human culture through word use throughout time periods.<sup id="cite_ref-Zimmer_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zimmer-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> This program has fallen under criticism because of errors in the metadata used in the program.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Content_issues_and_criticism">Content issues and criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Google_Books&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Content issues and criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The project has received criticism that its stated aim of preserving orphaned and out-of-print works is at risk due to scanned data having errors and such problems not being solved.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Users can report errors in Google scanned books at <i><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://support.google.com/books/partner/troubleshooter/2983879">support.google.com/books/partner/troubleshooter/2983879</a></i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scanning_errors">Scanning errors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Google_Books&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Scanning errors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hand_of_Google.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Hand_of_Google.png/220px-Hand_of_Google.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="276" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Hand_of_Google.png/330px-Hand_of_Google.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Hand_of_Google.png/440px-Hand_of_Google.png 2x" data-file-width="673" data-file-height="845" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hand_of_Google.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A hand scanned in a Google book</div></div></div>
<p>The scanning process is subject to errors. For example, some pages may be unreadable, upside down, or in the wrong order. Scholars have even reported crumpled pages, obscuring thumbs and fingers, and smeared or blurry images.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> On this issue, a declaration from Google at the end of scanned books says:
</p>
<table class="cquote pullquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: &#39;Times New Roman&#39;, Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">“
</td>
<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">The digitization at the most basic level is based on page images of the physical books. To make this book available as an ePub formated file we have taken those page images and extracted the text using Optical Character Recognition (or OCR for short) technology. The extraction of text from page images is a difficult engineering task. Smudges on the physical books' pages, fancy fonts, old fonts, torn pages, etc. can all lead to errors in the extracted text. Imperfect OCR is only the first challenge in the ultimate goal of moving from collections of page images to extracted-text based books. Our computer algorithms also have to automatically determine the structure of the book (what are the headers and footers, where images are placed, whether text is verse or prose, and so forth).
<p>Getting this right allows us to render the book in a way that follows the format of the original book. Despite our best efforts you may see spelling mistakes, garbage characters, extraneous images, or missing pages in this book. Based on our estimates, these errors should not prevent you from enjoying the content of the book. The technical challenges of automatically constructing a perfect book are daunting, but we continue to make enhancements to our OCR and book structure extraction technologies.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
</td>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: &#39;Times New Roman&#39;, Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">”
</td></tr>

</tbody></table>
<p>As of 2009 Google stated that they would start using <a href="/wiki/ReCAPTCHA" title="ReCAPTCHA">ReCAPTCHA</a> to help fix the errors found in Google Book scannings. This method would only improve scanned words that are hard to recognize because of the scanning process and cannot solve errors such as turned pages or blocked words.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Errors_in_metadata">Errors in metadata</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Google_Books&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Errors in metadata">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Scholars have frequently reported rampant errors in the <a href="/wiki/Metadata" title="Metadata">metadata</a> information on Google Books – including misattributed authors and erroneous dates of publication. <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Nunberg" title="Geoffrey Nunberg">Geoffrey Nunberg</a>, a linguist researching on the changes in word usage over time noticed that a search for books published before 1950 and containing the word "internet" turned up an unlikely 527 results. Woody Allen is mentioned in 325 books ostensibly published before he was born. Google responded to Nunberg by blaming the bulk of errors on the outside contractors.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Other metadata errors reported include publication dates before the author's birth (e.g. 182 works by Charles Dickens prior to his birth in 1812); incorrect subject classifications (an edition of <i>Moby Dick</i> found under "computers", a biography of Mae West classified under "religion"), conflicting classifications (10 editions of Whitman's <i>Leaves of Grass</i> all classified as both "fiction" and "nonfiction"), incorrectly spelled titles, authors, and publishers (<i>Moby Dick: or the White "Wall"</i>), and metadata for one book incorrectly appended to a completely different book (the metadata for an 1818 mathematical work leads to a 1963 romance novel).<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><dl><dd>"A review of the author, title, publisher, and publication year metadata elements for 400 randomly selected Google Books records was undertaken. The results show 36% of sampled books in the digitization project contained metadata errors. This error rate is higher than one would expect to find in a typical library online catalog.".<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<dl><dd><dl><dd>"The overall error rate of 36.75% found in this study suggests that Google Books’ metadata has a high rate of error. While “major” and “minor” errors are a subjective distinction based on the somewhat indeterminate concept of “findability”, the errors found in the four metadata elements examined in this study should all be considered major."<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<p>Metadata errors based incorrect scanned date makes research using the Google Books Project database difficult. Google has shown only limited interest in cleaning up these errors.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language_issues">Language issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Google_Books&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Language issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some European politicians and intellectuals have criticized Google's effort on <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_imperialism" title="Linguistic imperialism">linguistic imperialism</a> grounds. They argue that because the vast majority of books proposed to be scanned are in English, it will result in disproportionate representation of natural languages in the digital world. German, Russian, French, and Spanish, for instance, are popular languages in scholarship. The disproportionate online emphasis on English, however, could shape access to historical scholarship, and, ultimately, the growth and direction of future scholarship. Among these critics is <a href="/wiki/Jean-No%C3%ABl_Jeanneney" title="Jean-Noël Jeanneney">Jean-Noël Jeanneney</a>, the former president of the <i><a href="/wiki/Biblioth%C3%A8que_nationale_de_France" title="Bibliothèque nationale de France">Bibliothèque nationale de France</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Google_Books_versus_Google_Scholar">Google Books versus Google Scholar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Google_Books&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Google Books versus Google Scholar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While Google Books has digitized large numbers of journal back issues, its scans do not include the metadata required for identifying specific articles in specific issues. This has led the makers of <a href="/wiki/Google_Scholar" title="Google Scholar">Google Scholar</a> to start their own program to digitize and host older journal articles (in agreement with their publishers).<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Library_partners">Library partners</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Google_Books&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Library partners">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Google Books Library Project is aimed at scanning and making searchable the collections of several major research <a href="/wiki/Libraries" class="mw-redirect" title="Libraries">libraries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Along with <a href="/wiki/Bibliographic" class="mw-redirect" title="Bibliographic">bibliographic</a> information, snippets of text from a book are often viewable. If a book is out of <a href="/wiki/Copyright" title="Copyright">copyright</a> and in the public domain, the book is fully available to read or <a href="/wiki/Download" title="Download">download</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Google_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Google-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In-copyright books scanned through the Library Project are made available on Google Books for snippet view. Regarding the quality of scans, Google acknowledges that they are "not always of sufficiently high quality" to be offered for sale on Google Play. Also, because of supposed technical constraints, Google does not replace scans with higher quality versions that may be provided by the publishers.<sup id="cite_ref-books-help_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books-help-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The project is the subject of the <i><a href="/wiki/Authors_Guild_v._Google" class="mw-redirect" title="Authors Guild v. Google">Authors Guild v. Google</a></i> lawsuit, filed in 2005 and ruled in favor of Google in 2013, and again, on appeal, in 2015.
</p><p>Copyright owners can claim the rights for a scanned book and make it available for preview or full view (by "transferring" it to their Partner Program account), or request Google to prevent the book text from being searched.<sup id="cite_ref-books-help_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books-help-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The number of institutions participating in the Library Project has grown since its inception.<sup id="cite_ref-gblp1_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gblp1-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Initial_partners">Initial partners</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Google_Books&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Initial partners">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Google_Book_Search_-_notice_board_at_michigan_university_library.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Google_Book_Search_-_notice_board_at_michigan_university_library.jpg/220px-Google_Book_Search_-_notice_board_at_michigan_university_library.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Google_Book_Search_-_notice_board_at_michigan_university_library.jpg/330px-Google_Book_Search_-_notice_board_at_michigan_university_library.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Google_Book_Search_-_notice_board_at_michigan_university_library.jpg/440px-Google_Book_Search_-_notice_board_at_michigan_university_library.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Google_Book_Search_-_notice_board_at_michigan_university_library.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Notice about the project at Michigan University Library</div></div></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University_Library" class="mw-redirect" title="Harvard University Library">Harvard University Library</a><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<dl><dd><dl><dd>The Harvard University Library and Google conducted a pilot throughout 2005. The project continued, with the aim of increasing online access to the holdings of the Harvard University Library, which includes more than 15.8 million volumes. While physical access to Harvard's library materials is generally restricted to current Harvard students, faculty, and researchers, or to scholars who can come to Cambridge, the Harvard-Google Project has been designed to enable both members of the Harvard community and users everywhere to discover works in the Harvard collection.</dd></dl></dd></dl>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan" title="University of Michigan">University of Michigan</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan_Library" title="University of Michigan Library">University of Michigan Library</a><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<dl><dd><dl><dd>As of March 2012, 5.5 million volumes were scanned.<sup id="cite_ref-chronicle_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chronicle-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/New_York_Public_Library" title="New York Public Library">New York Public Library</a><sup id="cite_ref-nypl2004_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nypl2004-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<dl><dd><dl><dd>In this pilot program, NYPL is working with Google to offer a collection of its public domain books, which will be scanned in their entirety and made available for free to the public online. Users will be able to search and browse the full text of these works. When the scanning process is complete, the books may be accessed from both The New York Public Library's website and from the Google search engine.<sup id="cite_ref-nypl2004_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nypl2004-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bodleian_Library" title="Bodleian Library">Bodleian Library</a><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University_Libraries" title="Stanford University Libraries">Stanford University Libraries</a> (<a href="/wiki/Stanford_University_Libraries" title="Stanford University Libraries">SULAIR</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Additional_partners">Additional partners</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Google_Books&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Additional partners">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Other institutional partners have joined the project since the partnership was first announced:<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Austrian_National_Library" title="Austrian National Library">Austrian National Library</a><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bavarian_State_Library" title="Bavarian State Library">Bavarian State Library</a><sup id="cite_ref-libraryjournal2007_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-libraryjournal2007-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Biblioth%C3%A8que_municipale_de_Lyon" title="Bibliothèque municipale de Lyon">Bibliothèque municipale de Lyon</a><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Big_Ten_Academic_Alliance" title="Big Ten Academic Alliance">Big Ten Academic Alliance</a><sup id="cite_ref-btaa_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-btaa-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_Library_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Columbia University Library System">Columbia University Library System</a><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Complutense_University_of_Madrid" title="Complutense University of Madrid">Complutense University of Madrid</a><sup id="cite_ref-libraryjournal2007_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-libraryjournal2007-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University_Library" title="Cornell University Library">Cornell University Library</a><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ghent_University" title="Ghent University">Ghent University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ghent_University_Library" title="Ghent University Library">Ghent University Library</a>/<a href="/wiki/Boekentoren" title="Boekentoren">Boekentoren</a><sup id="cite_ref-libraryjournal2007_55-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-libraryjournal2007-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Keio_University" title="Keio University">Keio University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Keio_Media_Centers_(Libraries)" title="Keio Media Centers (Libraries)">Keio Media Centers (Libraries)</a><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/National_Library_of_Catalonia" class="mw-redirect" title="National Library of Catalonia">National Library of Catalonia</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Biblioteca_de_Catalunya" class="mw-redirect" title="Biblioteca de Catalunya">Biblioteca de Catalunya</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University_Library" title="Princeton University Library">Princeton University Library</a><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/University_of_California" title="University of California">University of California</a>, <a href="/wiki/California_Digital_Library" title="California Digital Library">California Digital Library</a><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/University_of_Lausanne" title="University of Lausanne">University of Lausanne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cantonal_and_University_Library_of_Lausanne" title="Cantonal and University Library of Lausanne">Cantonal and University Library of Lausanne</a><sup id="cite_ref-libraryjournal2007_55-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-libraryjournal2007-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/University_of_Mysore" title="University of Mysore">University of Mysore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mysore_University_Library" title="Mysore University Library">Mysore University Library</a><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:2_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_at_Austin" title="University of Texas at Austin">University of Texas at Austin</a>, University of Texas Libraries<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<dl><dd><dl><dd>The partnership was for digitizing the library's Latin American collection – about half a million volumes.<sup id="cite_ref-chronicle_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chronicle-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia" title="University of Virginia">University of Virginia</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia_Library" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Virginia Library">University of Virginia Library</a><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison" title="University of Wisconsin–Madison">University of Wisconsin–Madison</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison#Libraries" title="University of Wisconsin–Madison">University of Wisconsin Libraries</a><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<dl><dd><dl><dd>As of March 2012, about 600,000 volumes had been scanned.<sup id="cite_ref-chronicle_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chronicle-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Google_Books&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><b>2002</b>: A group of team members at Google officially launch the “secret ‘books’ project.”<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.com_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Google founders <a href="/wiki/Sergey_Brin" title="Sergey Brin">Sergey Brin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Larry_Page" title="Larry Page">Larry Page</a> came up with the idea that later became Google Books while still graduate students at Stanford in 1996. The history page on the Google Books website describes their initial vision for this project: “in a future world in which vast collections of books are digitized, people would use a ‘<a href="/wiki/Web_crawler" title="Web crawler">web crawler</a>’ to index the books’ content and analyze the connections between them, determining any given book's relevance and usefulness by tracking the number and quality of citations from other books.”<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.com_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> This team visited the sites of some of the larger digitization efforts at that time including the Library of Congress’s <a href="/wiki/American_Memory" title="American Memory">American Memory Project</a>, <a href="/wiki/Project_Gutenberg" title="Project Gutenberg">Project Gutenberg</a>, and the Universal Library to find out how they work, as well as the University of Michigan, Page’s alma mater, and the base for such digitization projects as <a href="/wiki/JSTOR" title="JSTOR">JSTOR</a> and Making of America. In a conversation with the at that time University President <a href="/wiki/Mary_Sue_Coleman" title="Mary Sue Coleman">Mary Sue Coleman</a>, when Page found out that the University’s current estimate for scanning all the library’s volumes was 1,000 years, Page reportedly told Coleman that he “believes Google can help make it happen in six."<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.com_72-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>2003</b>: The team works to develop a high-speed scanning process as well as software for resolving issues in odd type sizes, unusual fonts, and "other unexpected peculiarities."<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.com_72-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>December 2004</b>: Google signaled an extension to its Google Print initiative known as the Google Print Library Project.<sup id="cite_ref-gblp1_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gblp1-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Google announced partnerships with several high-profile university and public libraries, including the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan" title="University of Michigan">University of Michigan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard</a> (<a href="/wiki/Harvard_University_Library" class="mw-redirect" title="Harvard University Library">Harvard University Library</a>), <a href="/wiki/Stanford" class="mw-redirect" title="Stanford">Stanford</a> (<a href="/wiki/Green_Library" class="mw-redirect" title="Green Library">Green Library</a>), <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxford University">Oxford</a> (<a href="/wiki/Bodleian_Library" title="Bodleian Library">Bodleian Library</a>), and the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Public_Library" title="New York Public Library">New York Public Library</a>. According to press releases and university librarians, Google planned to digitize and make available through its Google Books service approximately 15&#160;million volumes within a decade. The announcement soon triggered controversy, as publisher and author associations challenged Google's plans to digitize, not just books in the public domain, but also titles still under copyright.
</p><p><b>September–October 2005</b>: Two lawsuits against Google charge that the company has not respected <a href="/wiki/Copyright" title="Copyright">copyrights</a> and has failed to properly compensate authors and publishers. One is a class action suit on behalf of authors (Authors Guild v. Google, Sept. 20 2005) and the other is a civil lawsuit brought by five large publishers and the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_American_Publishers" title="Association of American Publishers">Association of American Publishers</a>. (<a href="/w/index.php?title=McGraw_Hill_v._Google&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="McGraw Hill v. Google (page does not exist)">McGraw Hill v. Google</a>, Oct. 19 2005)<sup id="cite_ref-LAWSUITS_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LAWSUITS-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>November 2005</b>: Google changed the name of this service from Google Print to Google Book Search.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Its program enabling publishers and authors to include their books in the service was renamed Google Books Partner Program,<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> and the partnership with libraries became <a href="#Google_Books_Library_Project">Google Books Library Project</a>.
</p><p><b>2006</b>: Google added a "download a pdf" button to all its out-of-copyright, public domain books. It also added a new browsing interface along with new "About this Book" pages.<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.com_72-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>August 2006</b>: The <a href="/wiki/University_of_California_System" class="mw-redirect" title="University of California System">University of California System</a> announced that it would join the Books digitization project. This includes a portion of the 34&#160;million volumes within the approximately 100 libraries managed by the System.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>September 2006</b>: The <a href="/wiki/Complutense_University_of_Madrid" title="Complutense University of Madrid">Complutense University of Madrid</a> became the first Spanish-language library to join the Google Books Library Project.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>October 2006</b>: The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison" title="University of Wisconsin–Madison">University of Wisconsin–Madison</a> announced that it would join the Book Search digitization project along with the <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_Historical_Society" title="Wisconsin Historical Society">Wisconsin Historical Society</a> Library. Combined, the libraries have 7.2&#160;million holdings.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>November 2006</b>: The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia" title="University of Virginia">University of Virginia</a> joined the project. Its libraries contain more than five million volumes and more than 17&#160;million manuscripts, rare books and archives.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>January 2007</b>: The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Texas_at_Austin" title="University of Texas at Austin">University of Texas at Austin</a> announced that it would join the Book Search digitization project. At least one million volumes would be digitized from the university's 13 library locations.
</p><p><b>March 2007</b>: The <a href="/wiki/Bavarian_State_Library" title="Bavarian State Library">Bavarian State Library</a> announced a partnership with Google to scan more than a million public domain and out-of-print works in German as well as English, French, Italian, Latin, and Spanish.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>May 2007</b>: A book digitizing project partnership was announced jointly by Google and the <a href="/wiki/Cantonal_and_University_Library_of_Lausanne" title="Cantonal and University Library of Lausanne">Cantonal and University Library of Lausanne</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>May 2007</b>: The <a href="/wiki/Boekentoren" title="Boekentoren">Boekentoren</a> Library of <a href="/wiki/Ghent_University" title="Ghent University">Ghent University</a> announced that it would participate with Google in digitizing and making digitized versions of 19th century books in the French and Dutch languages available online.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>May 2007:</b> Mysore University announces Google will digitize over 800,000 books and manuscripts–including around 100,000 manuscripts written in Sanskrit or Kannada on both paper and palm leaves.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>June 2007</b>: The <a href="/wiki/Committee_on_Institutional_Cooperation" class="mw-redirect" title="Committee on Institutional Cooperation">Committee on Institutional Cooperation</a> (rebranded as the <a href="/wiki/Big_Ten_Academic_Alliance" title="Big Ten Academic Alliance">Big Ten Academic Alliance</a> in 2016) announced that its twelve member libraries would participate in scanning 10&#160;million books over the course of the next six years.<sup id="cite_ref-btaa_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-btaa-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>July 2007</b>: <a href="/wiki/Keio_University" title="Keio University">Keio University</a> became Google's first library partner in <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> with the announcement that they would digitize at least 120,000 public domain books.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>August 2007</b>: Google announced that it would digitize up to 500,000 both copyrighted and public domain items from <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University_Library" title="Cornell University Library">Cornell University Library</a>. Google would also provide a digital copy of all works scanned to be incorporated into the university's own library system.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>September 2007</b>: Google added a feature that allows users to share snippets of books that are in the public domain. The snippets may appear exactly as they do in the scan of the book, or as plain text.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>September 2007</b>: Google debuted a new feature called "My Library" which allows users to create personal customized libraries, selections of books that they can label, review, rate, or full-text search.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>December 2007</b>: <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a> was added as a partner in digitizing public domain works.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>May 2008</b>: <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> tapered off and planned to end <a href="/wiki/Live_Search_Books" title="Live Search Books">its scanning project</a>, which had reached 750,000 books and 80&#160;million journal articles.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>October 2008</b>: A <a href="/wiki/Google_Book_Search_Settlement_Agreement" title="Google Book Search Settlement Agreement">settlement</a> was reached between the publishing industry and Google after two years of negotiation. Google agreed to compensate authors and publishers in exchange for the right to make millions of books available to the public.<sup id="cite_ref-LAWSUITS_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LAWSUITS-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>November 2008</b>: Google reached the 7&#160;million book mark for items scanned by Google and by their publishing partners. 1&#160;million were in full preview mode and 1&#160;million were fully viewable and downloadable public domain works. About five million were <a href="/wiki/Out-of-print_book" title="Out-of-print book">out of print</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pcworldscan_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pcworldscan-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>December 2008</b>: Google announced the inclusion of magazines in Google Books. Titles include <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Magazine" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Magazine">New York Magazine</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Ebony_(magazine)" title="Ebony (magazine)">Ebony</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Popular_Mechanics" title="Popular Mechanics">Popular Mechanics</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>February 2009</b>: Google launched a mobile version of Google Book Search, allowing iPhone and Android phone users to read over 1.5 million public domain works in the US (and over 500,000 outside the US) using a mobile browser. Instead of page images, the plain text of the book is displayed.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>May 2009</b>: At the annual <a href="/wiki/BookExpo" class="mw-redirect" title="BookExpo">BookExpo</a> convention in New York, Google signaled its intent to introduce a program that would enable publishers to sell digital versions of their newest books direct to consumers through Google.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>December 2009</b>: A French court shut down the scanning of copyrighted books published in France, saying this violated copyright laws. It was the first major legal loss for the scanning project.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>April 2010</b>: Visual artists were not included in the previous lawsuit and settlement, are the plaintiff groups in another lawsuit, and say they intend to bring more than just Google Books under scrutiny. "The new class action," read the statement, "goes beyond Google's Library Project, and includes Google's other systematic and pervasive infringements of the rights of photographers, illustrators and other visual artists."<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>May 2010</b>: It was reported that Google would launch a digital book store called <a href="/wiki/Google_Editions" class="mw-redirect" title="Google Editions">Google Editions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>  It would compete with Amazon, Barnes &amp; Noble, Apple and other electronic book retailers with its own e-book store. Unlike others, Google Editions would be completely online and would not require a specific device (such as kindle, Nook, or iPad).
</p><p><b>June 2010</b>: Google passed 12 million books scanned.<sup id="cite_ref-PCWORLD_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PCWORLD-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>August 2010</b>: It was announced that Google intends to scan all known existing 129,864,880 books within a decade, amounting to over 4 billion <a href="/wiki/Digital_page" class="mw-redirect" title="Digital page">digital pages</a> and 2 trillion words in total.<sup id="cite_ref-PCWORLD_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PCWORLD-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>December 2010</b>: Google eBooks (Google Editions) was launched in the US.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>December 2010</b>: Google launched the Ngram Viewer, which collects and graphs data on word usage across its book collection.<sup id="cite_ref-Zimmer_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zimmer-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>March 2011</b>: A federal judge rejected the <a href="/wiki/Google_Book_Search_Settlement_Agreement" title="Google Book Search Settlement Agreement">settlement</a> reached between the publishing industry and Google.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>March 2012</b>: Google passed 20 million books scanned.<sup id="cite_ref-LP_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LP-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Howard20120309_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Howard20120309-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>March 2012</b>: Google reached a settlement with publishers.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>January 2013</b>: The documentary <i><a href="/wiki/Google_and_the_World_Brain" title="Google and the World Brain">Google and the World Brain</a></i> was shown at the <a href="/wiki/Sundance_Film_Festival" title="Sundance Film Festival">Sundance Film Festival</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>November 2013</b>: Ruling in <i><a href="/wiki/Authors_Guild_v._Google" class="mw-redirect" title="Authors Guild v. Google">Authors Guild v. Google</a></i>, US District Judge Denny Chin sides with Google, citing fair use.<sup id="cite_ref-Ars_Technica_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ars_Technica-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> The authors said they would appeal.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>October 2015</b>: The appeals court sided with Google, declaring that Google did not violate copyright law.<sup id="cite_ref-reuters_2015_10_16_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuters_2015_10_16-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> According to the New York Times, Google has scanned more than 25 million books.<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>April 2016</b>: The US Supreme Court declined to hear the Authors Guild's appeal, which means the lower court's decision stood, and Google would be allowed to scan library books and display snippets in search results without violating the law.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Status">Status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Google_Books&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Google has been quite secretive regarding its plans on the future of the Google Books project. Scanning operations had been slowing down since at least 2012, as confirmed by the librarians at several of Google's partner institutions. At University of Wisconsin, the speed had reduced to less than half of what it was in 2006. However, the librarians have said that the dwindling pace could be a natural result of maturation of the project – initially stacks of books were entirely taken up for scanning whereas now Google only needed to consider the ones that have not been scanned already.<sup id="cite_ref-chronicle_49-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chronicle-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The company's own Google Books <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/intl/en/googlebooks/about/history.html">history page</a> ends in 2007, and the Google Books blog was merged into the Google Search blog in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-wired_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wired-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite winning the decade-long litigation in 2017, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Atlantic" title="The Atlantic">The Atlantic</a></i> has said that Google has "all but shut down its scanning operation."<sup id="cite_ref-atlantic_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlantic-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> In April 2017, <i><a href="/wiki/Wired_(magazine)" title="Wired (magazine)">Wired</a></i> reported that there were only a few Google employees working on the project, and new books were still being scanned, but at a significantly lower rate. It commented that the decade-long legal battle had caused Google to lose its ambition.<sup id="cite_ref-wired_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wired-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_issues">Legal issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Google_Books&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Legal issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Authors_Guild_v._Google" class="mw-redirect" title="Authors Guild v. Google">Authors Guild v. Google</a></div>
<p>Through the project, library books were being digitized somewhat indiscriminately regardless of copyright status, which led to a number of lawsuits against Google. By the end of 2008, Google had reportedly digitized over seven million books, of which only about one million were works in the public domain. Of the rest, one million were in copyright and in print, and five million were in copyright but out of print. In 2005, a group of authors and publishers brought a major class-action lawsuit against Google for infringement on the copyrighted works. Google argued that it was preserving "orphaned works" – books still under copyright, but whose copyright holders could not be located.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Authors_Guild" title="Authors Guild">Authors Guild</a> and <a href="/wiki/Association_of_American_Publishers" title="Association of American Publishers">Association of American Publishers</a> separately sued Google in 2005 for its book project, citing "massive <a href="/wiki/Copyright_infringement" title="Copyright infringement">copyright infringement</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Google countered that its project represented a <a href="/wiki/Fair_use" title="Fair use">fair use</a> and is the digital age equivalent of a <a href="/wiki/Card_catalog" class="mw-redirect" title="Card catalog">card catalog</a> with every word in the publication indexed.<sup id="cite_ref-LAWSUITS_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LAWSUITS-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The lawsuits were consolidated, and eventually a <a href="/wiki/Google_Book_Search_Settlement_Agreement" title="Google Book Search Settlement Agreement">settlement was proposed</a>. The settlement received significant criticism on a wide variety of grounds, including antitrust, privacy, and inadequacy of the proposed classes of authors and publishers. The settlement was eventually rejected,<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> and the publishers settled with Google soon after. The Authors Guild continued its case, and in 2011 their proposed <a href="/wiki/Class_certification" class="mw-redirect" title="Class certification">class was certified</a>. Google appealed that decision, with a number of <a href="/wiki/Amicus_curiae" title="Amicus curiae">amici</a> asserting the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Inadequacy_of_the_class&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Inadequacy of the class (page does not exist)">inadequacy of the class</a>, and the Second Circuit rejected the <a href="/wiki/Class_certification" class="mw-redirect" title="Class certification">class certification</a> in July 2013, remanding the case to the District Court for consideration of Google's <a href="/wiki/Fair_use" title="Fair use">fair use</a> defense.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2015 Authors Guild filed another appeal against Google to be considered by the 2nd U.S. Circuit Court of Appeals in New York. Google won the case unanimously based on the argument that they were not showing people the full texts but instead snippets, and they are not allowing people to illegally read the book.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> In a report, courts stated that they did not infringe on copyright laws, as they were protected under the fair use clause.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Authors Guild tried again in 2016 to appeal the decision and this time took their case to be considered by the Supreme Court. The case was rejected, leaving the Second Circuit's decision on the case intact, meaning that Google did not violate copyright laws.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> This case also set a precedent for other case similar in regards to fair use laws as it further clarified the law and expands it. Such clarification is important in the new digital age as it affects other scanning projects similar to Google.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Other lawsuits followed the Authors Guild's lead. In 2006 a German lawsuit, previously filed, was withdrawn.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
In June 2006, Hervé de la Martinière,<sup id="cite_ref-Sage_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sage-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
a French publisher known as La Martinière and <a href="/wiki/%C3%89ditions_du_Seuil" title="Éditions du Seuil">Éditions du Seuil</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
announced its intention to sue Google France.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
In 2009, the Paris Civil Court awarded 300,000 <a href="/wiki/EUR" class="mw-redirect" title="EUR">EUR</a> (approximately 430,000 <a href="/wiki/USD" class="mw-redirect" title="USD">USD</a>) in damages and interest and ordered Google to pay 10,000 EUR a day until it removes the publisher's books from its database.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
The court wrote, "Google violated author copyright laws by fully reproducing and making accessible" books that Seuil owns without its permission<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_123-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> and that Google "committed acts of breach of copyright, which are of harm to the publishers".<sup id="cite_ref-Sage_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sage-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Google said it will appeal.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_123-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Syndicat National de l'Edition, which joined the lawsuit, said Google has scanned about 100,000 French works under copyright.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_123-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2009, Chinese author <a href="/wiki/Mian_Mian" title="Mian Mian">Mian Mian</a> filed a civil lawsuit for $8,900 against Google for scanning her novel, <i>Acid Lovers</i>. This is the first such lawsuit to be filed against Google in China.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
Also, in November that year, the China Written Works Copyright Society (CWWCS) accused Google of scanning 18,000 books by 570 Chinese writers without authorization. Google agreed on Nov 20 to provide a list of Chinese books it had scanned, but the company refused to admit having "infringed" copyright laws.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In March 2007, Thomas Rubin, associate general counsel for copyright, trademark, and trade secrets at Microsoft, accused Google of violating copyright law with their book search service. Rubin specifically criticized Google's policy of freely copying any work until notified by the copyright holder to stop.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Google licensing of public domain works is also an area of concern due to using of <a href="/wiki/Digital_watermarking" title="Digital watermarking">digital watermarking</a> techniques with the books. Some published works that are in the public domain, such as all <a href="/wiki/Copyright_status_of_work_by_the_U.S._government" class="mw-redirect" title="Copyright status of work by the U.S. government">works created by the U.S. Federal government</a>, are still treated like other works under copyright, and therefore locked after 1922.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Similar_projects">Similar projects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Google_Books&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Similar projects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Project_Gutenberg" title="Project Gutenberg">Project Gutenberg</a> is a volunteer effort to digitize and archive cultural works, to "encourage the creation and distribution of eBooks". It was founded in 1971 by Michael S. Hart and is the oldest digital library. As of October&#160;3,&#32;2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Google_Books&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, Project Gutenberg reached 50,000 items in its collection.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Internet_Archive" title="Internet Archive">Internet Archive</a> is a non-profit which digitizes over 1000 books a day, as well as mirrors books from Google Books and other sources. As of May&#160;2011<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Google_Books&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, it hosted over 2.8 million public domain books, greater than the approximate 1 million public domain books at Google Books.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Open_Library" title="Open Library">Open Library</a>, a sister project of Internet Archive, lends 80,000 scanned and purchased commercial ebooks to the visitors of 150 libraries.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/HathiTrust" title="HathiTrust">HathiTrust</a> maintains HathiTrust Digital Library since October 13, 2008,<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> which preserves and provides access to material scanned by Google, some of the Internet Archive books, and some scanned locally by partner institutions. As of May&#160;2010<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Google_Books&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, it includes about 6 million volumes, over 1 million of which are public domain (at least in the US).</li>
<li>Microsoft funded the scanning of 300,000 books to create <a href="/wiki/Live_Search_Books" title="Live Search Books">Live Search Books</a> in late 2006. It ran until May 2008, when the project was abandoned<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> and the books were made freely available on the Internet Archive.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/National_Digital_Library_of_India" title="National Digital Library of India">National Digital Library of India</a> (NDLI) is a project under Ministry of Human Resource Development, India. The objective is to integrate several national and international digital libraries in one single web-portal. The NDLI provides free of cost access to many books in English and the Indian languages.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Europeana" title="Europeana">Europeana</a> links to roughly 10 million digital objects as of 2010<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Google_Books&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, including video, photos, paintings, audio, maps, manuscripts, printed books, and newspapers from the past 2,000 years of European history from over 1,000 archives in the European Union.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gallica" class="mw-redirect" title="Gallica">Gallica</a> from the French National Library links to about 4,000,000 digitized books, newspapers, manuscripts, maps and drawings, etc. Created in 1997, the digital library continues to expand at a rate of about 5000 new documents per month. Since the end of 2008, most of the new scanned documents are available in image and text formats. Most of these documents are written in French.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wikisource" title="Wikisource">Wikisource</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Runivers" title="Runivers">Runivers</a></li></ul>

(January 2006). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20070403083647/http://www.ala.org/ala/alonline/techspeaking/2006columnsa/techJan2006.cfm">"Is This the Renaissance or the Dark Ages?"</a>. <i>American Libraries</i>. <a href="/wiki/American_Library_Association" title="American Library Association">American Library Association</a>. Archived from <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.ala.org/ala/alonline/techspeaking/2006columnsa/techJan2006.cfm">the original</a> on 2007-04-03<span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-130"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-130">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The number of Public Domain books at Google Books can be calculated by looking at the number of Public Domain books at <a href="/wiki/HathiTrust" title="HathiTrust">HathiTrust</a>, which is the academic mirror of Google Books. As of May 2010 <a href="/wiki/HathiTrust" title="HathiTrust">HathiTrust</a> had over 1 million Public Domain books.</span>
